- **References:** `<ef4d6964-015c-4d64-9b67-778466796f24@t26g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <bUofl.155284$se4.63263@en-nntp-03.dc1.easynews.com> <982e07bb-5a61-4c41-87c3-481dc102679d@f13g2000yqj.googlegroups.com> <go6dndjbdsEDOeLUnZ2dnUVZ_hidnZ2d@earthlink.com> <s8Ofl.211950$jv1.4305@en-nntp-09.dc1.easynews.com> <98865be6-6dde-4125-9966-39727336d86b@g39g2000pri.googlegroups.com>`

```
Pakku wrote:
> On Jan 27, 8:02 pm, "William M. Klein" <wmkl...@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
> wrote:
…[61 more quoted lines elided]…
> 'some' calling programs, not all.

Some don't like ENTRY usage, but I think it's a useful way to enhance an 
existing hunk of code. Our IO routines have multiple entry points, here's a 
skeleton:

PROCEDURE DIVISION USING MSTR-RECORD.
GOBACK.

FILE-OPEN.
ENTRY 'MA-OP' USING MSTR-RECORD.
  (code to open)
  MOVE 'Y' TO FILE-OPEN
  GOBACK

FILE-CLOSE.
ENTRY 'MA-CL' USING MSTR-RECORD.
  (code to close)
  MOVE 'N' TO FILE-OPEN
  GOBACK.

GET-EQUAL.
ENTRY 'MA-GE' USING MSTR-RECORD.
  IF FILE-OPEN NOT = 'Y'
     PERFORM FILE-OPEN.
(code to get key equal)
  GOBACK.

UPDATE-RECORD.
ENTRY 'MA-UP' USING MSTR-RECORD
  IF FILE-OPEN NOT = 'Y'
     PERFORM FILE-OPEN.
  PERFORM GET-EQUAL.
(code to update record)
  GOBACK.


In your case, give the above code sample a try. It a) might work, and/or b) 
you might like it.
```

## Thread: Cheap Uggs — 1 msgs

### Cheap Uggs

- **From:** ufoer2008@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2009-02-03T20:16:48-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<793a7315-2c3d-4f6a-9306-5e1096893a21@x16g2000prn.googlegroups.com>`

```
Uggs refer to a style of casual unisex boot, historically worn by
Australian surfers for the comfort and warmth after spending hours
waiting in the cold sea for a wave. The classic Ugg boot is
characterized by a simple construction of soft sheepskin suede, wide
footbase, round toe, wide mid-calf shaft with no lacing, and shearling
lining the interior of the boot. Constructed with a lightweight,
synthetic sole with a soft shank and no heel, the classic Ugg boot is
known for its featherweight comfort and spacious toebox. The Ugg is a
non-traditional boot, as the shearling acts as a foot lining,
obviating the need for a sock.
The natural properties of shearling results in thermostatic benefits.
Dense, fine-filament, fleecy fibers form the inner lining of the
boots, allowing air to circulate and keep the feet at body
temperature. Synthetic and faux fur boots do not have these purported
thermal properties; hence, sheepskin boots are highly prized for their
thermal qualities.

http://www.cheap-uggs.net/
```

<a id="t-f25555a06d"></a>
## Thread: %100 CPU Utilisation Problem — 8 msgs

### %100 CPU Utilisation Problem

- **From:** eselick <eselick@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2009-02-04T07:40:59-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b41046ee-2eb9-4df1-ab0f-c6ee13372d85@v5g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi
I'm working with an older version of Micro Focus in Windows - Object
COBOL (32-bit) Version 4.0.38 and  I compile individual program into
"gnt" or "int" files rather than distributing linked exe files.
When I run programs doing sequential reads or writes on large files
I'm getting %100 utilization on single core machines and %50 on my
own
dual core machine.
I may be running many of these processes on the same machine in the
near future and I need to find a way to reduce the CPU load.
Based on my experience with other languages I had thought that the
high numbers were due to not returning control to the OS system
periodically so that it could handle outstanding events.
I was delighted when I found references to the CBL_YIELD_RUN_UNIT and
PC_WIN_YIELD MF library routines which are supposed to and I quote
from the MF documentation " enable Windows to process any outstanding
events and gives any other programs in the system a chance to run. If
this is not done, Windows appears to freeze until the COBOL program
next yields control."
I've tried both of these routines and they don't seem to be doing
anything in this case; utilization is still at %50 on my machine.
I've also tried the following code which only works with my "ints"
and
gives a routine not found error message with "gnts"
              move 100 TO SLEEP-TIME
             CALL "COB32API"
      $SET CASE
            CALL WINAPI "Sleep" USING BY VALUE SLEEP-TIME
      $SET NOCASE
In any case it seems to be at best a partial solution. When the
"sleep
" command executes utilization goes to around %35 and then goes right
back to %50.
Does anyone know if it's possible to solve this problem in Micro
Focus. It must be common enough in many Cobol applications running in
Windows
Thanks in advance
Elliot
```

#### ↳ Re: %100 CPU Utilisation Problem

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2009-02-04T11:19:24-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<M6kil.9880$hc1.9053@flpi150.ffdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<b41046ee-2eb9-4df1-ab0f-c6ee13372d85@v5g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`

```
"eselick" <eselick@gmail.com> wrote in message 
news:b41046ee-2eb9-4df1-ab0f-c6ee13372d85@v5g2000prm.googlegroups.com...
> Hi
> I'm working with an older version of Micro Focus in Windows - Object
> COBOL (32-bit) Version 4.0.38 .....

> gives a routine not found error message with "gnts"
>              move 100 TO SLEEP-TIME
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> back to %50.

> Does anyone know if it's possible to solve this problem in Micro
> Focus. It must be common enough in many Cobol applications running in
> Windows

Actually it's common to programs running under Windows REGARDLESS of source 
language if the program does not process messages; that is, if it's a 'batch 
type processing' program containing "tight loops" ..  eg. read a record, 
modify a record, write a record.

However, I'm not so sure I would call it a "problem" to fully utilize your 
computer hardware.  That said, programs with "tight loops" which eat CPU 
time tend to have a negative impact on all the OTHER processes running on 
the box.

The most common adjustment to these programs if the CPU utilization is 
deemed a problem is the one you have found: yielding the balance of your 
time slice with the Sleep function at logical points within the processsing, 
e.g., after processing each single record.  BTW, you don't show the value of 
SLEEP-TIME; if this value is zero control is yielded to other threads of the 
same priority (if not in a wait state); if non-zero, control is 
unconditionally yielded to other threads for *at least*  the value of 
SLEEP-TIME.

So, you may get better overall performance by setting 'some value' of 
SLEEP-TIME. Sorry, there is no magic formula or algorithm to use. If this 
application is widely distributed you might consider making this value a 
"user option" or something you read from an INI file to allow  tuning 
without recompilation.

Another solution is to modify the thread priority (see the SetThreadPriority 
function) to a lower priority; after all, this IS a 'batch' process, right? 
(but remember how 'Sleep' works vis-a-vis to whom and for how long control 
is yielded).

If the process is I-O intensive, Asynchorous I-O could be an avenue of 
approach as well, as Asynch I-O automatically involves yielding control to 
other threads of execution.

Pre-emptive multi-tasking such as exists under Windows/32  came well after 
COBOL was created, so it should not be a surprise to anyone that several - 
or many - "accepted COBOL programming techinques"  don't play well with 
certain operating systems.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: %100 CPU Utilisation Problem

- **From:** eselick <eselick@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2009-02-04T12:34:56-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3fe5431e-62cd-46e6-8200-921983352849@a12g2000pro.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi Michael
First, thanks for the long and informative reply.

I've using thread.sleep() in Java where one of our programs was in a
hard loop and CPU utilization was also at %100. Once the thread.sleep
() call was made, utilization dropped radically - effectively to zero.
I guess I'm still wondering why a similar call in Cobol is not having
the same effect.

In any case, I have two questions:

1) How would I call the SetThreadPriority function in Micro Focus
Cobol?
2) I've implementing asynchronous processing on Unix and the AS400 via
the operating system facilities. But how would you do this in Windows
Micro Focus Cobol?

Thanks again
Elliot
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: %100 CPU Utilisation Problem

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-02-04T15:59:34-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<cdoil.66711$pp1.36708@en-nntp-06.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<b41046ee-2eb9-4df1-ab0f-c6ee13372d85@v5g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <M6kil.9880$hc1.9053@flpi150.ffdc.sbc.com> <3fe5431e-62cd-46e6-8200-921983352849@a12g2000pro.googlegroups.com>`

```
For asynchronous processing, check out the MF "chain" statement (as I recall).

When you say your files are large, are they > 2G?  The version of MF that you 
are using pre-dates MF's support for those and I can imagine that this MIGHT be 
part of your problem.  (However, if, given all the CPU resources, it does 
process the files correctly, that probably is a non-issue.)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: %100 CPU Utilisation Problem

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2009-02-04T16:55:51-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<67pil.10208$pr6.10183@flpi149.ffdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<b41046ee-2eb9-4df1-ab0f-c6ee13372d85@v5g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <M6kil.9880$hc1.9053@flpi150.ffdc.sbc.com> <3fe5431e-62cd-46e6-8200-921983352849@a12g2000pro.googlegroups.com>`

```
"eselick" <eselick@gmail.com> wrote in message 
news:3fe5431e-62cd-46e6-8200-921983352849@a12g2000pro.googlegroups.com...
> Hi Michael
> First, thanks for the long and informative reply.

Well, not having written a line of COBOL since 2001, and still reading the 
newgroup, it is nice to know I'm not the next Captain Dunsel. (If you didn't 
get that reference, I'm certain you can 'Google' for it. Include 'Star Trek' 
in your search terms).

> 1) How would I call the SetThreadPriority function in Micro Focus Cobol?

Same way you'd call any Windows' API  function. You can use the 
pseudo-handle obtained from GetCurrentThread(), which is probably the 
easiest way.

> 2) I've implementing asynchronous processing on Unix and the AS400 via
> the operating system facilities. But how would you do this in Windows
> Micro Focus Cobol?

I was not referring to asynchronous processing, I was referring to 
asynchronous I-O (also called :"overlapped I-O")  WITHIN the process. . But 
this is kind of esoteric anyway. Can't really do a whole lot with it without 
making your application multi-threaded.

A cheap out could be to simply  SetPriorityClass() against the entire 
PROCESS. Lower priority = less CPU time.

(And forget about doing just about any of this stuff on Win/9x, most of it 
works only on an NT-class O/S)

Windows API function documentation A-Z: 
http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/aa383688(VS.85).aspx
```

#### ↳ Re: %100 CPU Utilisation Problem

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-04T12:59:43-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8503fb4b-12d9-4921-af8f-463fbcb61949@i20g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<b41046ee-2eb9-4df1-ab0f-c6ee13372d85@v5g2000prm.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Feb 5, 4:40 am, eselick <esel...@gmail.com> wrote:
> Hi
> I'm working with an older version of Micro Focus in Windows - Object
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> Elliot

I suspect that the version of MF was designed to be used with Windows
95/98. PC_WIN_YIELD and such was required because Windows worked using
a message queue. In win3.x there was a single message queue for all
processes and not yielding locked up every process. Win 95/98 was
supposed to fix this but all Win3.x programs ran in a single process,
and Win32 (ie Win95/98) programs should have run in their own process.

I find it interesting that it can use 100% while reading disk files.
In rational systems a request for a disk transfer (at the block level)
is handed off to the OS and the program uses no CPU time while the
disk transfer takes place, even if this is just drive cache to RAM
cache, and the OS gives this time to another program to use the CPU.

The implication of 100% is that the program is idling in a loop using
CPU time even when the next record it is wanting to process is sitting
on the disk drive and the drive heads are moving to the track (10ms)
and/or waiting for latency (6ms).

This is what I associate with MS-DOS programs in the 80s that sit in a
keyhit() loop using 100% CPU because 'what else could use it?'.
Fortunately I used multi-user/multi-tasking systems that detected this
behaviour and catered for it.

It may well be that the run-time is checking for keyhit() or similar.

What happens if every, say, 100 records, you display a message on the
console, perhaps the record key or a counter ?

If, however, the read-ahead is working perfectly and the 100%
represents actual processing load (rather than busy-wait) then
reducing the CPU load to 50% would double the run time.

The best solution may be to not use Windows.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: %100 CPU Utilisation Problem

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2009-02-05T08:35:41-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<TPCil.3698$PE4.3497@nlpi061.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<b41046ee-2eb9-4df1-ab0f-c6ee13372d85@v5g2000prm.googlegroups.com> <8503fb4b-12d9-4921-af8f-463fbcb61949@i20g2000prf.googlegroups.com>`

```
"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:8503fb4b-12d9-4921-af8f-463fbcb61949@i20g2000prf.googlegroups.com...
On Feb 5, 4:40 am, eselick <esel...@gmail.com> wrote:

>I suspect that the version of MF was designed to be used with Windows
>95/98. PC_WIN_YIELD and such was required because Windows worked using
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>and Win32 (ie Win95/98) programs should have run in their own process.

"Grab and hold CPU"  also occurs when running any "no message queue" 
program such as MS-DOS programs, even on NT-Class machines, unless you 
explicitly "Yield" (WIn/3) or "Sleep" (Win/NT) .

It also occurs with sloppy GUI programming which tries to do something which 
takes "too long" DURING the processing of a notification message.

Much of Win/9x uses a 16-bit GDI subsystem, which would explain  why "Win/32 
(9x) did not fix the problem noted with Win/16 (3.x)."   Win/Nt+ are the 
only 'pure-32-bit' Windows' Operating Systems.  However, Win/3x was designed 
to support cooperative multi-tasking, not preemptive multi-tasking.  Maybe 
this mismatch between GDI and multi-tasking design the real problem?

>The best solution may be to not use Windows.

There is a callable MS-DOS service to yield the rest of the time slice when 
running under WIndows.  I think it may be a subfunction of the 0x2F 
multiplex interrupt. That might be worth searching for. Or maybe not, since 
this is a Windows' program (I think).

Or, you could maybe identify the exact problem routine and develop (or 
engage someone to develop for you) a dynamic-link library to replace 
whatever function in the MF-runtime is eating up CPU and call that function 
instead of calling the COBOL intrinsics.  Avoid the MF-runtime, avoid the 
blockage.
```

<a id="t-83940c10ed"></a>
## Thread: COBOL delegates and .NET Action<string> — 19 msgs

_Topics: [`Language features and syntax`](topics.md#syntax)_

### COBOL delegates and .NET Action<string>

- **From:** biblepolyglotte@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2009-02-06T08:26:42-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ac023df1-75da-4b8b-a263-513a82a46977@g1g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hello group,

As a first project in OO COBOL, I've been porting Jon Skeet's C#
snippet compiler, Snippy, to Micro Focus Object COBOL. Once ported, I
plan to add support for compiling COBOL snippets. After that comes the
Reflector add-in. :)

MainWindow.xaml.cbl and Commands.cbl are done. ParagraphWriter.cbl is
also nearly complete, but for the life of me, I can't figure out how
to port C# delegates and .NET Actions to COBOL.

MF Object COBOL clearly does support the delegate-id paragraph, and
I've even seen a delegate example in this group's archive. So this
should be easy, but I'm still missing something.

Would anyone be willing to show how a COBOL delegate would be used
port this C# snippet?

  using System;
  // ...
  using System.Windows.Documents;
  using System.Windows.Threading;

  namespace Snippy
  {
    class ParagraphWriter : TextWriter
    {
      /// <summary>
      /// Equivalent of Action in .NET 3.5
      /// </summary>
      delegate void VoidAction();

      Paragraph paragraph;

      // ...

      public override void Write(string text)
      {
        VoidAction action = () => paragraph.Inlines.Add(new Run
(text));
        paragraph.Dispatcher.BeginInvoke(DispatcherPriority.Normal,
action);
      }

    }
  }

-----

Skeet's XML comment above suggests using the .NET 3.5 Action type. So
the Write function could be rewritten in C# as follows -- and I'd also
like to know how to do this in COBOL:

  public override void Write(string text)
  {
    Action<string> deed = AddRun;
    paragraph.Dispatcher.BeginInvoke(DispatcherPriority.Normal, deed,
text); // <---
  }

  public void AddRun(string text)
  {
    paragraph.Inlines.Add(new Run(text));
  }
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL delegates and .NET Action<string>

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-07T09:09:41+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6v3jnrFhu56lU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<ac023df1-75da-4b8b-a263-513a82a46977@g1g2000pra.googlegroups.com>`

```
biblepolyglotte@gmail.com wrote:
> Hello group,
>
…[61 more quoted lines elided]…
>  }

I'd like to take time to comment on this but I simply don't have any right 
now :-)

As far as COBOL delegates go, there is no such direct facility in standard 
COBOL, but you could obtain the same effect by using procedure pointers. The 
use of these may be non-standard, but I believe MF implement this facility 
in their COBOL. I think Fujitsu also has support for it but I've never used 
it. I realise this is an academic exercise, but I have to ask why bother? 
The facilities are in C#; use C#...

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL delegates and .NET Action<string>

- **From:** biblepolyglotte@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2009-02-06T15:28:56-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<369ae740-7323-41fd-a6f1-ffdce718c27f@b16g2000yqb.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<ac023df1-75da-4b8b-a263-513a82a46977@g1g2000pra.googlegroups.com> <6v3jnrFhu56lU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Thanks for the reply about procedure pointers. I thought that feature
was provided by the (non-standard) delegate-id paragraph. Either I'm
on the wrong track, or it's just a matter of grasping the syntax. Any
further tips or code samples would be appreciated.

> I realise this is an academic exercise, but I have to ask why bother?
> The facilities are in C#; use C#...

Professionally I work in C# and VB, and my development team also uses C
++. But I'm fascinated by COBOL and COBOL.NET.

As they say in Amsterdam, "COBOL makes happy". Just look at that
picture of Mike Murach.

Now, I've seen a few of your posts in favor of C#, and I understand
your point of view. C# is at the forefront of language development.

Why then, when late at night C# and VB are both coming out my ears, do
I burn the midnight oil for COBOL.NET?

The pragmatic reason is that it provides an exercise for I've already
learned about .NET, and along the way I'm forced to learn a little bit
more about the framework, the CLS, the CTS, and the CLR. But the real
reason is this:

All who use COBOL, are forever speaking its name.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL delegates and .NET Action<string>

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-08T00:19:26+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6v5922Fi945fU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<ac023df1-75da-4b8b-a263-513a82a46977@g1g2000pra.googlegroups.com> <6v3jnrFhu56lU1@mid.individual.net> <369ae740-7323-41fd-a6f1-ffdce718c27f@b16g2000yqb.googlegroups.com>`

```
biblepolyglotte@gmail.com wrote:
> Thanks for the reply about procedure pointers. I thought that feature
> was provided by the (non-standard) delegate-id paragraph. Either I'm
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> All who use COBOL, are forever speaking its name.

I made a living from it for over two decades and have been writing it for 
more than 4. It was the first high level language I learned and I fell in 
love with it.

(I still enjoy writing it and was writing some today, but I have no 
illusions about it. It is like Mediaeval monks moving from Latin to English. 
Latin is still a useful language scholastically, and it will always be part 
of our heritage, but you won't hear a lot of it in the corner pub, or the 
supermarket... If you want to DO stuff in the REAL world, you need 
English...If you want to DO stuff in the IT world you need Objects and 
Layers.... Monolithic procedural code is nothing more than a curiosity or a 
Batch solution.)

But that was then, and this is now. The world has moved on. We don't have 
centralised mainframes driving green screens as our only option any more. OO 
COBOL and the .NET implementations are amazing pieces of software 
engineering but the user community failed to embrace them 15 years ago when 
they should have if COBOL was to have a chance in the 21st century. (To be 
fair, there were other pressures at work too, and I don't hold the user 
community entirely responsible. Nevertheless, a lack of foresight and 
general unwillingness to embrace change was a major factor in COBOL being 
left on the jetty after the steamboat sailed.)

Nostaligia is fine for Acadaemia, where the rent is paid by a grant, but in 
the hard nosed world of business things have moved beyond closed datasets 
and procedural processing.

COBOL is labour intensive. Labour costs money. It takes 4000 lines of it to 
do what can be done in 800 lines of C#. I recently converted a COBOL app to 
C#. The C# version is 12000 lines (still too much and I have learned in the 
process. If I were doing this again I could probably get it to 8000. The 
COBOL app was 70,000 and the C# app does more, looks better, and is MUCH 
more fun to work with. Collections, iterations, enumerations, Tree Views, 
complex GUI components, all flow easily and just work. Need a stack? there 
is a pre-defined collection complete with Push, POP, and Peek methods... I 
remember writing this in COBOL and taking a day to get it working correctly 
for all cases. C#, 10 minutes... Support is on line and immediate, there are 
free video tutorials to help you learn at your own pace and the IDE is light 
years ahead of COBOL.

Standard COBOL doesn't play well with other languages in OO environments. 
(OO COBOL is good, but it is too verbose...) COBOL for .NET is an expensive 
non-sequitur.  Writing .NET apps in COBOL is like putting a tuxedo on a 
chimp.

But mostly, the COBOL community just doesn't get it. There are people there 
who still think that converting their apps to .NET makes them automatically 
modernized. The essential concepts of Objects and Layers are simply ignored 
or dismissed.The ground work in object technology isn't there (and is 
generally scoffed at by people who have never written an OO program in COBOL 
or any other language) and the results are like wading through treacle, 
running a Ferrari on compost, or heading for the moon in a hot air balloon. 
It is kind of an impasse... without proper Object knowledge OO COBOL is 
useless; when people acquire proper Object knowledge, they realise that the 
COBOL implementation of OO is less streamlined than other languages like C# 
or Java (which also happen to be free and there is no problem getting people 
who can write them).

OK, I promised myself 15 minutes for this and I've had 25, so time's up... 
:-)

One man's (pretty unpopular) opinion... :-)

Check out procedure pointers to implement delegates, and good luck with your 
experiments. In a week or two when I have some clear space, I'll take a look 
at this and see if I can post some samples, but right now I have no chance.

Pete.
```

```
that you SHOULD be using a relatively current Micro Focus or Fujitsu compiler 
(not the Visual COBOL of the OP) to try it.

Both vendors have products targeted at that environment.

Then, again, these are COMMERCIAL products and they are not cheap. I certainly 
don't think either is intended for "playing around".  I don't know if there are 
30-cay trial versions of either, but you might want to try and find out *IF* the 
only goal is to see how COBOL does things that C# can.
```

```
I don't see any expiration date for the NetExpress Personal Edition.
It does have a few limitations, e.g., no more than 2,200 lines of code
per program. But for new development in object-oriented COBOL it is a
Ferrari, period. FxCop would never let anyone get away with a class a
class longer than 2,200 lines.

With Mr. Skeet's permission, granted today, I've published the source
code for Add One Snippy:

http://www.codeplex.com/AddOneSnippy

This COBOL version of Snippy is pre-beta, and doesn't yet compile
anything. But a number of features do work.

The 50th anniversary of COBOL should be celebrated with complete
amnesia about the letter B. COBOL this year is simply Common Object
Oriented Language: COOL.

There were a few reasons on December 31, 2008 to think "that ship has
sailed". But in 2009 we party. This is the year for needless migration
to COBOL, and uninhibited new development.

The business landscape in 2010 will look very different, when billions
of new lines of open source object-oriented COBOL are in circulation.
Get busy.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL delegates and .NET Action<string>

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-10T01:17:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6val7lFis7bgU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<ac023df1-75da-4b8b-a263-513a82a46977@g1g2000pra.googlegroups.com> <6v3jnrFhu56lU1@mid.individual.net> <369ae740-7323-41fd-a6f1-ffdce718c27f@b16g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <33f231d8-3776-411e-a05f-24354034bcd2@w39g2000prb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Rene_Surop wrote:
>> All who use COBOL, are forever speaking its name.
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> all.

Or... you could just see computer languages as tools to achieve a goal, and 
not as religious dogma to be defended at all costs.

There's nothing wrong with COBOL. It is an excellent language. It was 
conceived in generosity, with a spirit that transcended individual interests 
of the parties concerned. But it belongs to a different age.

The problem is not with the language but with the paradigm. The future 
belongs to objects and layers. COBOL was extended (by some amazingly 
foresighted software engineers) to encompass objects, but nobody wanted to 
know. Now it is too late. Other languages implement the object paradigm 
better than COBOL and that's all there is to it. It isn't about what C# or 
Java can do and whether it can be done in COBOL; it is about how easily and 
quickly it can be done in a language best suited to the task.

COBOL will remain very useful for things like Batch processing (as long as 
that remains necessary... I believe it (batch processing) will be extinct 
within 10 - 15 years as more powerful processors with multi cores come on 
line and can do everything in real time. When you have Business models which 
are true analogues of a business, in real time, and are capable of 
re-constructing any events that have occurred at any time in the history of 
the business, batch processing is unnecessary. The way in which we do 
business will change also, just as it changed from the Victorian model to 
our current models.)

COBOL, (largely due to its simple construction and English-like syntax) has 
appeal for many people, but it is not a Programmer's language, in the 
computer science sense of the word.

Yes, you can write programs in it, but they are procedural programs for a 
former time, when things were different. The changes coming in this century 
will make COBOL even more anachronistic than it is already.

Having said all of that, it is likely to be around for a while yet. I 
predicted it would not be in use  by 2015, in 1997. I now think that may be 
arguable. Certainly it will never have the ascendancy it once enjoyed as 
"the only game in Town" back in the late 70s and early 80s, but I don't 
think it will completely disappear. OO COBOL has a future as a means of 
moving Legacy systems to newer environments and as a means of enabling 
existing COBOL programmers to transition into OOP,but, as I mentioned 
elsewhere, once the OO paradigm is grasped, the limitations in OO COBOL 
become apparent. It isn't that you CAN'T do stuff in OO COBOL (you mostly 
can...) it is just that it is more labour intensive than doing it in other 
languages which are now available. As a major development language, I think 
my 2015 prediction will not be far off the mark, but there is almost 
certainly going to be continuing work on legacy, even then.

Most people here will think I am anti-COBOL. I'm not. (I'm happy to use it 
for jobs where it is ideally suited, and I do. I also have a fairly large 
chest of OO COBOL components which I developed over years and won't be 
replacing any time soon...). But I do believe it is not relevant to the 
environments in which I wish to work.

(In passing, I have just got home after deploying a very complex package 
written in C#, on a site outside NZ. The package has some COBOL components 
it uses. These were the ONLY part of it that caused me any trouble. If COBOL 
encountered an exception, it tried to dump. The dump could not run, so it 
displayed a meaningless message and blew the whole (.NET) environment 
away...Using things like COBOL DECLARATIVES to trap exceptions worked up to 
a point, but there were other difficulties with unmanaged code exceptions 
and InterOP services. I finally resolved it all, but it was work and took a 
number of hours I could ill afford. I learned from it, but as always, the 
fees charged by Experience are high... COBOL needs a TRY...CATCH facility 
which can be applied to any block of code... (no, actually it doesn't...just 
use a language that DOES have such a facility...)

Whether we like it or not, the following factors are contributing to its 
decline:

1. The skill base is being eroded by age, perception, and lack of training.
2. More and more companies are realising that it costs too much to maintain 
and opting for package solutions (which are getting better every year), 
and/or moving to client/server as a cheaper platform, where COBOL is not 
really relevant.
3. Legacy and history are driving most of the COBOL sites still in 
existence. How long can that continue?
4. It costs too much to develop and deploy. Only large companies can afford 
to continue running it. For SMEs it is really not an option.

>I like see IT in general peaceful though when integrated with
> each other, and YES I would prefer handling it in Cobol.

Sure, I'm all in favour of peace, and a personal preference is fine. But 
remember that a personal preference is based on intuition and emotion, not 
necessarily on the evidence.

Pete.
```

```
Some computer languages are like our native spoken language - we can
speak them fluently without changing how we think.   Often they are
languages we became fluent with early.   When we are away from them
for a few years, they come back instantly.    They are comfortable.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL delegates and .NET Action<string>

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-10T14:59:46+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6vc5ciFj63iqU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<ac023df1-75da-4b8b-a263-513a82a46977@g1g2000pra.googlegroups.com> <6v3jnrFhu56lU1@mid.individual.net> <369ae740-7323-41fd-a6f1-ffdce718c27f@b16g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <33f231d8-3776-411e-a05f-24354034bcd2@w39g2000prb.googlegroups.com> <trl0p41rqn1cnt13rn0n7dl168i81plsn8@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Sun, 8 Feb 2009 17:51:08 -0800 (PST), Rene_Surop
> <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com> wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> speak them fluently without changing how we think.

There's the nub of it.

In a simplistic centralised environment there is no need to change the way 
we think. Processes on the computer are sequential Von-Neumann type 
processes which emulate the human approach to the same problem.

In the real world somebody does a series of steps, takes a decision, does 
another series of steps, and iterates the process until a result is achieved 
(Dyjkstra had the insight to realise this and formalise it mathematically). 
Computer processes in the early days modelled what people did. Just as a 
person has one brain the computer had one core.

But as soon as you start networking there are are many cores available. They 
can work synchronously and/or asynchronously, can use their own data or 
share data with every core on the network, and it isn't just local it is 
world-wide. We are coming to a time where not only will there be many nodes 
on the network (each one with a processing core), but there will be many 
cores available to each node. This has implications which are not 
immediately obvious.

Objects allow easy "packaging" of information (which knows how to process 
itself and has various methods which define its behaviour) to be flashed 
around the network and present the same interface to every core it 
encounters. Layers allow selection and isolation of objects from and to 
various cores. Hence my statement that the "future belongs to objects and 
layers". Even from this very brief simplistic description, it is immediately 
obvious that this represents a fundamental change in the way we approach 
problem solving on a computer. In other words, there is a paradigm shift, 
and what was OK in the 1960s with a single processing core is way less than 
optimum in the 21st century with multiple cores available.

But the way we think about things (except for a very few of us) HASN'T 
changed. If you look at any specific part of the network model described 
above, you see data being processed by a core, and that seems right, 
familiar and comfortable, so there is apparently no reason to change the way 
things have traditionally been done. And you CAN achieve results that way, 
even in a networked environment.

BUT, if you make the  mental switch, understand the whole business with 
encapsulated objects (components) and layers, latch on to the idea of data 
which has behaviours built into it, that can process itself any way, 
anywhere, anytime there is a core available, then you can really make this 
paradigm sing and the results can be quite spectacular.

I've been talking about this to a team of COBOL people on a site which is 
migrating away from COBOL and dragging themselves into the 21st century 
(first step is migrating from ISAM to RDB...) They are already planning and 
looking forward to refactoring their existing COBOL into objects. I have 
given them tools which automatically convert their existing data access into 
an RDB with an Object Oriented Data Access Layer (various objects handle 
various table sets on their newly created RDBs),  so one layer in their 
triple tier target has been automatically achieved. The Business Logic and 
Presentation Layers will need manual intervention, but they are enthusiastic 
and fired up about doing it. I showed them some demos of what can be 
achieved with objects and layers and why this is important. When I left, 
they were a COBOL team with fire in their eyes and the talk was all of 
objects and layers. The Management of this site are wise, and are happy to 
invest in C# and Visual Studio training for the existing team. They have a 
long term plan which will give them a system running in .NET, totally Object 
Oriented, and using C# as the language of preference, BUT which has also 
salvaged around 80% of their existing COBOL investment.

(This is being achieved WITHOUT the need to purchase a COBOL compiler for 
.NET (CLR); there's little point in spending more money on a language you 
are moving away from...instead the money can be spent on training for 
Relational Data Base, Visual Studio, and C#)

(They are adopting the model I proposed a few months back which is 
documented here:
http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobdata/ShowMe.aspx?ptitle=Process flow 
diagram...&pcontent=images/MigrationProcessOverview.jpg )

It's a good result and I shall do everything I can to make sure they get 
there.

> Often they are
> languages we became fluent with early.   When we are away from them
> for a few years, they come back instantly.    They are comfortable.

Yes, and comfortability is often more important to people than actually 
maximising results. It's like new shoes. They are seldom as comfortable as 
the old ones... at first. But once they are broken in they become 
comfortable and suddenly you can run faster (or not... you know what I mean 
:-))

>
> Others don't come back quite so quickly when we are away from them for
> a while.   They didn't quite become native.

Yes, those are the Ugg boots and the winklepickers  :-)

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL delegates and .NET Action<string>

- **From:** biblepolyglotte@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2009-02-06T19:56:48-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c92d0aaa-4a1e-4056-b254-28fd628f07fc@t13g2000yqc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<ac023df1-75da-4b8b-a263-513a82a46977@g1g2000pra.googlegroups.com> <6v3jnrFhu56lU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I cobbled something together based on MF's DelegatesDemo and CobolDoKu
examples. The code compiles, which is progress.

With apologies for the bad COBOL style, here are the delegate
definition and the ParagraphWriter's Write(string) method:

   delegate-id. VoidAction as "AddOneSnippy.VoidAction".
   data division.
   linkage section.
   01  textRun  type "System.Windows.Documents.Run".
   procedure division using by value textRun.
   *> The method is just a prototype and should contain no
implementation.
   end delegate VoidAction.

   ---------------------------

   method-id. "Write" is public.
   data division.
   working-storage section.
   01  deed     type "AddOneSnippy.VoidAction".
   procedure division using s as string.
       *> Create a new delegate
       set deed to new
"AddOneSnippy.VoidAction"(outputParagraph::"Inlines"::"Add"(
           new "System.Windows.Documents.Run"(s)))
       *> Pass the delegate to a dispatcher
       invoke outputParagraph::"Dispatcher"::"BeginInvoke"(
           type
"System.Windows.Threading.DispatcherPriority"::"Normal", deed)
       .
   end method "Write".
```

```
http://www.codeproject.com/script/Surveys/VoteForm.aspx?srvid=891

survey presenters aware that COBOL is not dead yet... :-)

Pete.
```

### OT: Tragedy in Oz

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-10T15:51:43+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6vc8duFj0s63U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
We have had full coverage here of the fires in Victoria and there is a 
general feeling of sympathy and support.

Our PM has offered support to Kevin Rudd and I understand it has been 
accepted and 100 firefighters here are waiting to step in and fill the gaps 
when the volunteers who are working so hard over there have to go back to 
work. It may be a drop in the bucket but at least we are doing what we can. 
People here are shocked and appalled at what has happened.

It was heartbreaking to see interviews with some of the people who endured 
this and lost their homes and their loved ones.

Tragedies like Katrina, the Tsunami, earthquakes in Japan and elsewhere, 
flooding in China, and these terrible fires, not to mention the floods in 
Queensland which have also killed people, remind all of us how fragile we 
are against Nature and also how these adversities transcend national 
differences.

I'd like to express my condolences to anyone in CLC who has been touched by 
these events.

We can only hope this dreadful season in Victoria will be over soon without 
further loss of life.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCobol 8.0

- **From:** Rene_Surop <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2009-02-11T22:52:49-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<68b87ee0-a69e-4487-9f08-655e53200453@v5g2000pre.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<Agzkl.21026$Sj1.2760@newsfe09.iad> <6vgvloFjureaU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
>
> > I'm using PowerCobol 8.0 and MySql 5.1 with MySQL ODBC 3.51. To
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

In MF NetExpress, I am using this code;



       01  connString  pic x(256) value
            'Driver={MySQL ODBC 5.1 Driver};Server=192.168.0.5;'
           &'Port=3306;Database=inventory;User=usernm;'
           &'Password=mypass;Option=3;'.




           EXEC SQL
               CONNECT TO :connString
           END-EXEC.


Connection string of ADO or ODBC (all database) is in this website
reference;
           http://www.connectionstrings.com/

Good luck.


Rene
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCobol 8.0

- **From:** "Jude" <jude_robert@cox.net>

```
Thanks for the responses!

       "Database=avsdb;User=avssys;" delimited by size
       "Password=avssys;Option=3;" delimited by size into connString.

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCobol 8.0

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-13T21:44:22+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<6vkq77Fiuhc9U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<Agzkl.21026$Sj1.2760@newsfe09.iad> <6vgvloFjureaU1@mid.individual.net> <Iz5ll.31$aZ3.4@newsfe01.iad>`

```
Jude wrote:
> Thanks for the responses!
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> Not sure exactly what this means.

You cannot use a connection string in the way you have shown and expect it 
to work. The CONNECT is looking for a simple string to recognise the 
connection by:  MyDB1  or something like that. (I posted an example here a 
while back which used an instance stamp... point is it is has no meaning... 
it is just a "connection symbol".)

Fujitsu has been told to use an SQL information file and the file is not 
present. You need to change your COBOL85.CBR file and the ODBC.inf file it 
points at. Remove the SQLinfo line from COBOL85.CBR.

You also need to instantiate an instance of ADO before it can recognise your 
connection string and result set.

more below...
>
>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>>
 ...but you didn't, did you? :-)

How did you expect it to work?

Fujitsu supports ONLY ODBC access directly from their COBOL

If you want the power of non-ODBC access, you must use the Component Object 
Model (COM). Bear in mind that the rest of the computer community use 
components as a matter of course and  they EXPECT to have object instances 
of components doing the work for them. Only old-time COBOL programmers (OK, 
maybe PL/1 too...) see components and objects as the mark of Satan... :-)

I have used MySQL from Fujitsu COBOL (and found it to be an excellent RDB) 
but ONLY with the ODBC driver they provide, via the external environment 
information files.  However, I am pretty confident you can do this with ADO 
and, because you had a go and posted the code here, here's an untested 
sample that should be close to what you need:
...
             ENVIRONMENT     DIVISION.
             CONFIGURATION SECTION.
             REPOSITORY.
                    CLASS COM AS "*COM".

...
             01  ADO-connection pic x(128) value "ADODB.Connection".
             01  ADO-ResultSet   pic x(128) value "ADODB.Recordset".
             01  OBJ-ADO OBJECT REFERENCE COM.
             01  OBJ-RS     OBJECT REFERENCE COM.
             01  ADO-open-string pic x(256) value spaces.
             01  SQL-string           pic x(256) value spaces.
             01  ADO-RS-cursor-type   pic  s9(4) comp-5.
                    88  adOpenDynamic                 value 2.
             01  ADO-RS-lock-type pic s9(4) comp-5.
                    88  adLockOptimistic                value 3.
...
                    move spaces to ADO-open-string
                    string  "DRIVER={MySQL ODBC 3.51 
Driver};SERVER=judehome;"
                                     delimited by size
                               "DATABASE=avsdb;UID=root;"
                                     delimited by size
                                "PWD=avssys;OPTION=35;"
                                     delimited by size
                                          into ADO-open-string
                     end-string
       *>
       *>  instantiate connection and resultset objects...
       *>
                     invoke COM "CREATE-OBJECT"
                          using ADO-connection
                          returning OBJ-ADO
                     end-invoke
                     invoke COM "CREATE-OBJECT"
                           using ADO-ResultSet
                           returning OBJ-RS
                     end-invoke
       *>
       *>  Step through the above with the debugger and make sure that 
OBJ-ADO and OBJ-RS
       *>  have been loaded with an instance address (not NULL). If there is 
a problem with your
       *>  system (like, ADO is not installed or up to date...) you SHOULD 
get a COM abend.
       *> you can trap this with the special COM-EXCEPTION class (Check your 
Fujitsu docs...)
       *>
       *> all set, let's connect...
       *>
                      invoke OBJ-ADO "open"
                            using  ADO-open-string
                      end-invoke
       *>
       *>  It will abend if there is a problem. See above. (Abends are VERY 
rare, and the Fujitsu COM Class
       *>  generally passes back good information from the Automation 
interface.)
       *>

There are a number of ways you can choose to process.

The 2 main ones are using either a Command object or the Resultset object.

(As this is quick free help, I won't cover the command object; 
recordset/resultset processing is the most popular anyway...)

If you intend to use the recordset/resultset processing you will need to 
invoke methods on the  RS object.

This can be a bit tricky as there are quite a few possibilities. To get you 
started, here's an example...


       *> set up your query....
                      move "SELECT model, colour FROM showroom WHERE marque 
= 'Yugo'"
                            to SQL-string
                      set adOpenDynamic to TRUE
                      set adLockOptimistic to TRUE

       *>  get the result set...
                      invoke OBJ-RS "Open"
                            using  SQL-string,
                                      ADO-OBJ,
                                      ADO-RS-cursor-type,
                                      ADO-RS-lock-type
                      end-invoke
 Process it using the various commands available until it returns EOF...

I strongly recommend you take a look at the ADO command set and do a quick 
tutorial on ADO processing.

W3C schools are excellent.

http://www.w3schools.com/

Check out their "learn ADO" option.

HTH,

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCobol 8.0

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Rene_Surop <infodynamics_ph@yahoo.com>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7de0f498-239c-40e1-a31a-4b3a95bbf600@y38g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<Agzkl.21026$Sj1.2760@newsfe09.iad> <6vgvloFjureaU1@mid.individual.net> <Iz5ll.31$aZ3.4@newsfe01.iad> <6vkq77Fiuhc9U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
>
> You cannot use a connection string in the way you have shown and expect it
> to work. The CONNECT is looking for a simple string to recognise the
> connection by:  MyDB1  or something like that.
>

Never used Fujitsu Cobol, tried browsing the Fujitsu COBOL book a few
years back and tried encoding a few codes looking for some
alternatives for MF but it didn't work for me. The above code can be
executed with a few statements in Microfocus.

      $set sql(dbman=odbc, autocommit)


       01  connString  pic x(256) value
            'Driver={MySQL ODBC 5.1 Driver};Server=192.168.0.5;'
           &'Port=3306;Database=inventory;User=usernm;'
           &'Password=mypass;Option=3;'.


           EXEC SQL
               CONNECT TO :connString
           END-EXEC.


Using the EXEC SQL...END-EXEC statement, you can code fairly square
SQL command in between. MF uses compiler directive ($set) and it will
do the rest.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCobol 8.0

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-28T00:25:08+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<70q0sjFhsa5pU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<Agzkl.21026$Sj1.2760@newsfe09.iad> <6vgvloFjureaU1@mid.individual.net> <Iz5ll.31$aZ3.4@newsfe01.iad> <6vkq77Fiuhc9U1@mid.individual.net> <7de0f498-239c-40e1-a31a-4b3a95bbf600@y38g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <49A2D72D.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
Frank Swarbrick wrote:
>>>> On 2/17/2009 at 5:32 PM, in message
> <7de0f498-239c-40e1-a31a-4b3a95bbf600@y38g2000prg.googlegroups.com>,
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> secure it (the password, in any case)?

SOME programs DO have the cnnection string(s) hard coded in them.(These tend 
to be amateurish "first attempts"and as experience is gained, coding 
techniques improve...)

For Fujitsu (using ODBC) They are usually stored in an external file. 
Richard posted a sample here a little while back. As you can see, the 
pasword is encrypted.

In ASP.NET programs the connection strings are stored in a Web Config file. 
(They don't HAVE to be, but there are obvious advantages in doing so, and it 
is accepted practice for C#). Again you can encrypt them if you want.

Finally, connection strings CAN be embedded in code with user and pasword 
passed to them as runtime parameters (or not...) depending on programmer 
preference.

For dynamic connections with varying users and passwords, connection strings 
are built at runtime (usually by simple concatenation), although a 
"template" of the connection string may be obtained from an external source 
first, and then the variable parts slotted into it by a Regular Expression 
or static concatenation. For different access methods and RDBMS, the strings 
are often similar, but not identical. There are thousands of posts on the 
Net from people needing help to decide exactly WHICH format is correct for 
the flavour of their data source.

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCobol 8.0

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-17T18:25:42-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ae996996-363f-49d1-8d8a-f976e48f2abc@t39g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<Agzkl.21026$Sj1.2760@newsfe09.iad>`

```
On Feb 12, 1:11 am, "Jude" <jude_rob...@cox.net> wrote:
> Hi,
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Thanks!

This is Linux but it shows the principle.

You will also need to set up the ODBC config so that it knows how to
deal with the datasource (editest).

In file COBOL.CBR:
@ODBC_INF=EDISQL.INF

In file EDISQL.INF
[SQL_DEFAULT_INF]
@SQL_SERVER=Redhat
@SQL_DATASRC=editest
@SQL_USERID=deskware
@SQL_PASSWORD=PCHKJKOPGFMHPMGAIGNHHL
@SQL_ACCESS_MODE=READ_WRITE
@SQL_COMMIT_MODE=MANUAL
@SQL_CONCURRENCY=LOCK
@SQL_ODBC_CURSORS=USE_DRIVER
@SQL_QUERY_TIMEOUT=0
@SQL_DATASRC_KIND=MACHINE_DS

In cobol program:
       EXEC SQL CONNECT TO DEFAULT END-EXEC
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu NetCobol 8.0

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-02-18T10:58:08-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<e2d5e201-9bde-48b9-9545-45dd9eb919ce@y38g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<Agzkl.21026$Sj1.2760@newsfe09.iad> <ae996996-363f-49d1-8d8a-f976e48f2abc@t39g2000prh.googlegroups.com> <WQSml.4816$xQ5.2289@newsfe23.iad>`

```
On Feb 19, 1:05 am, "Jude" <jude_rob...@cox.net> wrote:
> Thanks!
>
> This is the way I connect, but it does require a system or user DSN. The
> solution that Rene_Surop would be great but I have not been able to get that
> to work with Fujitsu.

Fujitsu Cobol's SQL is built in and only does what the compiler
defines:

   CONNECT TO <servername> [AS connectionname]
        USER <username/password>
or
   CONNECT TO DEFAULT

The servername is required to be set up in the ODBC.INF file with the
details. The .INF file is just a text file so your program could write
it before trying the connect.

Alternately you could use a pre-processor like other cobols do and not
use the Fujitsu built-in syntax, or use a CALL/INVOKE interface to
something else.
```

```
"""There are three types of DSNs:
(1) System DSN -- can be used by anyone who has access to the machine.
DSN info is stored in the registry.
(2) User DSN -- created for a specific user. Also stored in the
registry.
(3) File DSN -- DSN info is stored in a text file with .DSN
extension.
"""

The point about 'system or user DSNs' (on Windows) is that they are
stored in the registry. With Fujitsu the connection can be made with a
text file which the program could write before attempting to make the
connection.


> I'm also curious as to how Redhat can be the server when he is connecting to
> MySQL. Does that work on Linux?

One of my machines is called 'RedHat' though it now runs another
variety of Linux.

Yes I run Fujitsu Cobol (7.3), MySQL and ODBC (UnixODBC) on Linux.
Also PostgreSQL.

There is no reason that I can see that would stop a Windows Fujitsu
program to access MySQL on Linux, or vice versa.
```

```
On Feb 20, 12:48 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Richard wrote:
> > On Feb 19, 4:33 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
…[50 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Years ago there was a large distinction. MySQL was easy to set up and
very fast on searching but did not have transactions. It was regarded
as being a lightweight server mainly for web servers (such as Google)
where there was little updating compared to searching.

PostgreSQL was the full featured database, slower but more reliable.

Now they are much closer with PostgreSQL improving its speed while
MySQL added transactions and other features (such as Oracle
compatibility). But this was done by adding complexity such as having
to choose the database type.

I've always preferred PostgreSQL for production.
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL Program Editor

```
"Chalao Adda" <chalao.adda@gmail.com> wrote in message 
news:49936714$0$90275$14726298@news.sunsite.dk...
>I have some COBOL programs and subprograms. I want to compile and link them 
>in one go. Which programming editor could do that? All the simple editors 
>can compile only one program at a time. I am looking for an editor on 
>Windows which could compile and link multiple programs at a time.

Many compiler products offer a 'command-line compile/link' capability.   So 
you write a *.CMD file to do many of these.  Many fine editor products (eg 
Ultra Edit) offer an 'execute command' function... so you execute your 
command file.

MCM
```

```
In most cases all you need is to extract the zip file to your eclipse
installation folder. The zip file contains a plugins and sometimes a
features folder, exactly as eclipse. When you start eclipse the next time,
the plugin will be added, but check compatibility first: Some plugins need a
specific eclipse version (e.g. Europa, Ganymede)

The best way to install a plugin is directly from its update site: Select
"Help -> Software updates". If the update site is not configuered, add the
new one (e.g. http://www.coboleditor.com/update/UnitStudio/) and download
the files directly. You find the upadte site urls on eclipse.org.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49949468$0$2847$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`

```
Davide Grandi a ï¿½crit :
> WITH Eclipse (maybe asking to the COBOL plugin mailing list before) :
> you had to use an ant script that launches the OpenCOBOL recompiles for
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>   
Eclipse is indeed a big peace of software.
For quick editing, I use PSPad or Notepad++, two good editors.

Hereunder, a small python script that will generate a makefile from a
list of your cbl and cob file in the directory where it is executed.
Run everytime you add a cobol file (using f.e. wingide).
You have then a make file with all dependencies. Edit a cobol source
file and use make. It's allways a good choice!

Regards

Alain
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL Program Editor

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** Alain Reymond <arwebmail@skynet.be>
- **Date:** 2009-02-12T22:29:02+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<499494a1$0$2847$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<49949468$0$2847$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`
- **References:** `<49936714$0$90275$14726298@news.sunsite.dk> <EJVkl.13590$as4.1203@nlpi069.nbdc.sbc.com> <499457e1$0$90272$14726298@news.sunsite.dk> <49945c9d$0$2851$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be> <1iv1pb2.3p1vae16x5y57N%davide.grandi@mclink.it> <49949468$0$2847$ba620e4c@news.skynet.be>`

```
Alain Reymond a ï¿½crit :
Oops: forgot the script!

#!/bin/env python

import os, sys, string, re

from optparse import OptionParser

LinuxOptions = "--fcompiler=cob"
WindowsOptions = "--compiler=mingw32 --fcompiler=cob"
supportedExtensions = [".cbl", ".cob"]

makeTemplate = """PROGRAM        = %(programName)s

COBCOMPILER    = cob
FFLAGS         = %(compilerOptions)s
LDFLAGS        =
LIBS           =

all: $(PROGRAM)

%(makeRules)s

ALL_SOURCE = %(allSource)s

clean:
    rm -rf *.int *.idy *.lst *.gnt

mrproper:
    rm -rf $(PROGRAM)

"""

def findProgramName(sourceFilename):

    line = sourceFilename.replace('.',' ')
    comps = line.split()
    if len(comps)==2:
        return comps[0].strip()
   
    return None

def findDepends(sourceFilename):

    sourceFile = file(sourceFilename, "r")
    lines = sourceFile.readlines()
    sourceFile.close()

    moduleDepends = []
    p=re.compile('copy')
    for line in lines:
        if p.search(line.lower())!=None:
            q=re.compile('"[a-zA-Z0-9./-_]+"')
            r = q.search( line.lower())
            if r:
                if r.group().strip('"') not in ('cbl_copy_file'):
                    moduleDepends.append(r.group().strip('"'))
    return moduleDepends

def isSupportedExtension(entry):
    for extension in supportedExtensions:
        if entry.find(extension)!=-1:
            return True
    return False

def findSupportedExtension(entry):
    for extension in supportedExtensions:
        if entry.find(extension)!=-1:
            return extension
    return ""


def generateMakefile(targetDir, inputProgramName, compilerOptions,
programSuffix):

    dirContents = os.listdir(targetDir)
    currDir = os.getcwd()
    os.chdir(targetDir)

    sourceFiles = []
    moduleSourceFiles = []
    objectFiles = {}
    programName = ""

    if sys.platform == "win32":
        CompilerOptions = WindowsOptions
    else:
        CompilerOptions = LinuxOptions

    for dirEntry in dirContents:
        if os.path.isfile(dirEntry):
            if isSupportedExtension(dirEntry):
                extension = findSupportedExtension(dirEntry)
                sourceFiles.append(dirEntry)
                testProgramName = findProgramName(dirEntry)
                depends = findDepends(dirEntry)
                objectFiles[dirEntry.split(".")[0]] = [depends, extension]

                if testProgramName!=None:
                    depends = findDepends(dirEntry)
                    programName = testProgramName

    makeRules = []

    if inputProgramName!="[Auto]":
        programName = inputProgramName

    for object in objectFiles.keys():

        extension = objectFiles[object][1]
        makeRules.append(object+".int: "+object+extension+" "+"
".join([obj for obj in objectFiles[object][0]]))

    for object in objectFiles.keys():

        extension = objectFiles[object][1]
        makeRules.append(object+".gnt: "+object+extension+" "+"
".join([obj for obj in objectFiles[object][0]]))
       
    makeRules.append("")
   
    makeRules.append("%.int : %.cbl")
    makeRules.append("\t$(COBCOMPILER) $(FFLAGS) $<")
    makeRules.append("%.gnt : %.cbl")
    makeRules.append("\t$(COBCOMPILER) $(FFLAGS) -u $<")

    makeRules.append("")   
   
    programName="prog"
    makeRules.append(programName+": "+"int gnt")

    makeRules.append("int:"+" ".join([object+".int" for object in
objectFiles.keys()]))
    makeRules.append("gnt:"+" ".join([object+".gnt" for object in
objectFiles.keys()]))

    sourceFilesString = " ".join(sourceFiles)
    moduleSourceFilesString = " ".join(moduleSourceFiles)
    makeRuleString = "\n".join(makeRules)

    makeFile = file("makefile", "w")
    makeFile.write(makeTemplate % {
        "programName":programName,
        "makeRules":makeRuleString,
        "allSource":sourceFilesString,
        "moduleSource":moduleSourceFilesString,
        "compilerOptions":compilerOptions,
        "programSuffix":programSuffix,
        "CompilerOptions":CompilerOptions}
    )
    makeFile.close()

    os.chdir(currDir)

def printUsage():
    pass

if __name__ == "__main__":

    # Display user interface if tkinter is installed and no arguments
are given, otherwise
    # run as command line tool.

    parser = OptionParser()
    parser.add_option("-t", "--target-dir", dest="targetDir",
default=".", help="Generate makefiles in DIR.", metavar="DIR")
    parser.add_option("-a", "--app-name", dest="appName",
default="[Auto]", help="Application name.")
    parser.add_option("-c", "--compiler-options",
dest="compilerOptions", default="-P -C \"CONSTANT LIEU 'BXL' \"",
help="Compiler options.")

    if sys.platform == "win32":
        parser.add_option("-s", "--program-suffix", default="exe",
dest="programSuffix", help="Executable suffix.")
    else:
        parser.add_option("-s", "--program-suffix", default="",
dest="programSuffix", help="Executable suffix.")

    parser.add_option("-g", "--gui", action="store_true", dest="useGui",
default=False, help="Show user interface. Requires Tkinter and Tcl/Tk.")

    (options, args) = parser.parse_args()

    if options.useGui:
        root = Tk()
        appWindow = AppWindow(root, targetDir=options.targetDir,
appName=options.appName, compilerOptions=options.compilerOptions,
programSuffix=options.programSuffix)
        root.mainloop()
        sys.exit(0)
    else:
        print "Generating makefiles.",
        generateMakefile(options.targetDir, options.appName,
options.compilerOptions, options.programSuffix)
        print "Done."
```

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2009-02-11T17:06:42-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<499305A2.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
>>> On 2/10/2009 at 9:23 PM, in message
<cdc05216-bd17-48af-9c77-50c70d08bdb6@q9g2000yqc.googlegroups.com>,
<biblepolyglotte@gmail.com> wrote:
> Micro Focus COBOL.NET does have the TRY...CATCH...FINALLY...END-TRY
> structure. I realize that not everyone will want to go there.
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> C# uses the += operator. Anyone know how that works in COBOL.NET?

Once upon a time I believe I had the answer to this, but I can't recall what
it is.  I think I used .NET Reflector to translate the C# .EXE from bytecode
back to C# and it showed that the actual method used by +=.

.NET Reflector is very fun anyway, so take a look:
http://www.red-gate.com/products/reflector/.
 
Frank
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL delegates and .NET Action<string>

- **From:** biblepolyglotte@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2009-02-12T08:32:13-08:00

```
On Feb 11, 7:06 pm, "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbr...@efirstbank.com>
wrote:
> Once upon a time I believe I had the answer to this, but I can't recall what
> it is.  I think I used .NET Reflector to translate the C# .EXE from bytecode
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> - Show quoted text -

I hadn't thought of using Reflector here, but the Snippy add-in for
Reflector is the next project I'd like to port from C# to COBOL. Then
I'd like to see if a Reflector add-in to disassemble from MSIL to
COBOL is feasible. :-)

I realized overnight that RoutedEventHandlers are automatically wired
up in generated COBOL files, when event handler attributes are added
to XAML elements in the designer. So I'll have a look at generated
COBOL first, but will keep Reflector in mind.

FxCop demanded a strong name for the AddOneSnippy assembly, so the
snippet compiler is now called COBOL the Barbarian:

http://www.codeplex.com/barbarian

The Reflector add-in to compile from C#, VB, C++, and COBOL -- and
browse the resulting MSIL -- will be COBOL the Conqueror. Now if only
there were a strong name for snippet.
```

```
William M. Klein wrote:

> doesn't help me, but maybe it should <G>

The full framework is around 300 MB. Normally it would not install all of 
that and what it does download is highly compressed, nevertheless, it is 
large.

Automatic Updates are kind of a two edged sword. For people who are not sure 
about configuring PCs it best to have it on, but as knowledge grows you may 
want to filter this a bit. I have mine set to notify me when updates are 
available and then I take them if they are relevant.

When you say it took a large hunk of your hard disk, you have to remember 
that 200 GB hard drives are pretty much "normal" these days (I have a laptop 
with more than that), so .3 GB is really not so tragic...

What does it get you?

Nothing at all if you are NOT using Windows software developed for .NET. 
If you have bought any recent applications (say, in the last 2 years) they 
are probably targeted at a .NET framework (read the box...). So, as a User, 
there is NOT a lot of gain in installing .NET, UNLESS you have a fairly new 
machine and are running fairly new applications, or intend to do so in the 
near future. Software that needs it will probably install it automatically 
anyway, as I did with the COBOL Structure tool. The problem then is that it 
won't be up to date. Between framework releases, this doesn't really matter 
too much, as your next automatic update will detect it is installed and 
update it for you.

When there is a major new release (as 3.5 is...) then the updates can be 
large. However, this doesn't happen every week... once a year is probably 
fair.

For you, Bill, you can visualize it as a large runtime system. It provides 
services and facilities that ensure improved security, less vulnerability, 
improved performance, and no more abends and BSODs. Applications which run 
in the framework are said to be "Managed". They CANNOT blow away your system 
and crash, are properly multithreaded, and behave properly. However, the 
framework can also run "Unmanaged" code (legacy and native code 
implementations) and these of course, CAN misbehave. Usually, the framework 
gives them what they need and then leaves them to it; it takes no 
responsibility for them.

From a DEVELOPER perspective, the view is quite different. The .NET 
framework provides a level playing field for components written in any 
language to interact seamlessly. COBOL can talk to C#, VB can interact with 
C++, and so on. Added to that you have around 70,000 fully developed and 
debugged Classes that you can use. Just about anything you could want is 
already there, all you have to do is connect the dots... Development is many 
times quicker than with COBOL (partly for that reason) and maintenance is 
much simpler.

But most importantly, the whole thing is MUCH more stable and it looks and 
feels better than standard Windows.

Should you run with Automatic Updates enabled?

For most people, the answer would be "yes". (Most of the updates are closing 
vulnerability or fixing known bugs, so you probably DO want them.)  However, 
if you are bothered by having resources consumed by updates you don't 
understand and may have no use for, then drop it down a notch and go for 
notification.

HTH,

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Microsoft .NET Framework 3.5 Service Pack 1 and .NET Framework 3.5 Family Update

- **From:** biblepolyglotte@gmail.com
- **Date:** 2009-02-13T14:28:31-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c402a03f-ea26-48ec-abbe-19bb97ced8a6@w34g2000yqm.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<1O2ll.52219$tg5.25831@en-nntp-05.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
I understand the concern. Even if disk space is cheap, a 300MB
download and update can take a machine hostage -- and nobody likes to
see a pagefile grow.

Most of the new .NET features sound like large changes. The changes to
native images and WPF sound like they should bring improved
performance for .NET applications running on your desktop. The ASP and
Click Once changes could bring benefits whenever you (very likely) use
ASP-driven websites or (possibly) install Click Once applications.

Interestingly, a significant number of early adopters for Framework
3.5 and Service Pack 1 have been COBOL.NET developers. I personally
know of at least one.

C# developers, on the other hand, have been slow to adapt. As Jon
Skeet reports in his book, a majority of C# developers are one or even
two major language versions behind the curve. Many of them are still
using Windows Forms.

Please, let's not mention the large number of VB6 developers who
rejected not only VB.NET, but even the final service pack for VB6 --
which was delivered on March 29, 2004.

 - Matt
----------------------------------
Matt Fisher
C# and VB6 applications developer
```

```
COBOL the Barbarian finally captures console output, and has been
upgraded to Beta status. Feature parity with Snippy, the C# original,
has been achieved and surpassed. Those of us who write C# and VB for
pay can now sneak a COBOL utility onto the job site.

Three weeks ago I only knew a little bit of procedural COBOL from
reading Murach. But COBOL has proven to be a highly accessible and
capable .NET language. The hard part has been deciphering WPF.

Hats off to the engineers at Micro Focus!
```

```
this type of thing.
```
